)\n         </span>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row" style="white-space:           </span>\n           )\n          </span>\n          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">\n           <a href="#cite_note-3">\n            [3]\n           </a>\n          </sup>\n         </div>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr style="display:none">\n        <td colspan="2">\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row" style="white-space: nowrap;">\n         <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">\n          Operating system\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/MacOS" title="MacOS">\n          macOS\n      </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row" style="white-space: nowrap;">\n         <a href="/wiki/Software_license" title="Software license">\n          License\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td>\n         <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_software" title="Proprietary software">\n          Proprietary\n         </a>\n         <a href="/wiki/Commercial_software" title="Commercial software">\n          commercial software\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        Microsoft PowerPoint\n       </b>\n       is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Presentation_program" title="Presentation program">\n        presentation program\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-lead_4-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Britannica-lead-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       created by Robert Gaskins and Dennis Austin\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-lead_4-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Britannica-lead-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       at a software company named Forethought, Inc.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-lead_4-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Britannica-lead-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It was released on April 20, 1987,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-5">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       initially for Macintosh computers only.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-lead_4-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Britannica-lead-4">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Microsoft acquired PowerPoint for $14\xc2\xa0million three months after it appeared.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-acquisition-lead_6-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Britannica-acquisition-lead-6">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This was Microsoft\'s first significant acquisition,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-lead_7-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-lead-7">\n         [7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and Microsoft set up a new business unit for PowerPoint in Silicon Valley where Forethought had been located.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-lead_7-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-lead-7">\n         [7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint became a component of the Microsoft Office suite, first offered in 1989 for Macintosh\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">\n        <a href="#cite_note-8">\n         [8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and in 1990 for Windows,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">\n        <a href="#cite_note-9">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       which bundled several Microsoft apps. Beginning with PowerPoint 4.0 (1994), PowerPoint was integrated into Microsoft Office development, and adopted shared common components and a converged user interface.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001-lead_10-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-lead-10">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint\'s market share was very small at first, prior to introducing a version for Microsoft Windows, but grew rapidly with the growth of Windows and of Office.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">\n        <a href="#cite_note-11">\n         [11]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (pp402\xe2\x80\x93404)\n       </sup>\n       Since the late 1990s, PowerPoint\'s worldwide market share of presentation software has been estimated at 95 percent.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-12">\n         [12]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint was originally designed to provide visuals for group presentations within business organizations, but has come to be very widely used in many other communication situations, both in business and beyond.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica-uses-lead_13-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Britannica-uses-lead-13">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The impact of this much wider use of PowerPoint has been experienced as a powerful change throughout society,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies_14-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Davies-14">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       with strong reactions including advice that it should be used less,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       should be used differently,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayer-Atkinson-2004_16-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Mayer-Atkinson-2004-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       or should be used better.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-2007_17-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-2007-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The first PowerPoint version (Macintosh 1987) was used to produce overhead transparencies,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead_18-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       the second (Macintosh 1988, Windows 1990) could also produce color 35mm slides.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead_18-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The third version (Windows and Macintosh 1992) introduced video output of virtual slideshows to digital projectors, which would over time completely replace physical transparencies and slides.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead_18-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-CACM-Gaskins-2007-lead-18">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       A dozen major versions since then have added many additional features and modes of operation\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001-lead_10-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-lead-10">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and have made PowerPoint available beyond Apple Macintosh and Microsoft Windows, adding versions for iOS, Android, and web access.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crowley_19-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Crowley-19">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <div class="toc" id="toc">\n       <div class="toctitle">\n        <h2>\n         Contents\n        </h2>\n       </div>\n       <ul>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1">\n         <a href="#History">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           1\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           History\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2">\n           <a href="#Creation_at_Forethought_.281984.E2.80.931987.29">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             1.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Creation at Forethought (1984\xe2\x80\x931987)\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3">\n           <a href="#Acquisition_by_Microsoft_.281987.E2.80.931992.29">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             1.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Acquisition by Microsoft (1987\xe2\x80\x931992)\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4">\n           <a href="#Part_of_Microsoft_Office_.28since_1993.29">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             1.3\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Part of Microsoft Office (since 1993)\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5">\n           <a href="#Sales_and_market_share">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             1.4\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Sales and market share\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6">\n         <a href="#Operation">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           2\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Operation\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7">\n         <a href="#Cultural_impact">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           3\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Cultural impact\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8">\n           <a href="#Business_uses">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Business uses\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9">\n           <a href="#Uses_beyond_business">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Uses beyond business\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10">\n           <a href="#Cultural_reactions">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.3\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Cultural reactions\n            </span>\n           </a>\n           <ul>\n            <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-11">\n             <a href="#Use_it_less">\n              <span class="tocnumber">\n               3.3.1\n              </span>\n              <span class="toctext">\n               Use it less\n              </span>\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-12">\n             <a href="#Use_it_differently">\n              <span class="tocnumber">\n               3.3.2\n              </span>\n              <span class="toctext">\n               Use it differently\n              </span>\n             </a>\n            </li>\n            <li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-13">\n             <a href="#Use_it_better">\n              <span class="tocnumber">\n               3.3.3\n              </span>\n              <span class="toctext">\n               Use it better\n              </span>\n             </a>\n            </li>\n           </ul>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14">\n           <a href="#U.S._military_excess">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.4\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             U.S. military excess\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15">\n           <a href="#Artistic_medium">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             3.5\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Artistic medium\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16">\n         <a href="#PowerPoint_Viewer">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           4\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           PowerPoint Viewer\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17">\n         <a href="#Versions">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           5\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Versions\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18">\n         <a href="#File_formats">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           6\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           File formats\n          </span>\n         </a>\n         <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19">\n           <a href="#Binary_.281987.E2.80.932007.29">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             6.1\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Binary (1987\xe2\x80\x932007)\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20">\n           <a href="#Office_Open_XML_.28since_2007.29">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             6.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Office Open XML (since 2007)\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21">\n         <a href="#See_also">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           7\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           See also\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22">\n         <a href="#References">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           8\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           References\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23">\n         <a href="#Further_reading">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           9\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Further reading\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24">\n         <a href="#External_links">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           10\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           External links\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n       </ul>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">\n        History\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Creation_at_Forethought_(1984\xe2\x80\x931987)">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Creation_at_Forethought_.281984.E2.80.931987.29">\n        Creation at Forethought (1984\xe2\x80\x931987)\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Creation at Forethought (1984\xe2\x80\x931987)">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint was created by\n       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gaskins" title="Robert Gaskins">\n        Robert Gaskins\n       </a>\n       and Dennis Austin at a software\n       <a href="/wiki/Startup_company" title="Startup company">\n        startup\n       </a>\n       in\n       <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">\n        Silicon Valley\n       </a>\n       named\n       <a href="/wiki/Forethought,_Inc." title="Forethought, Inc.">\n        Forethought, Inc.\n       </a>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">\n        <a href="#cite_note-20">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Forethought had been founded in 1983 to create an integrated environment and applications for future personal computers that would provide a graphical user interface, but it had run into difficulties requiring a "restart" and new plan.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brock-2017-re-Forethought_21-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Brock-2017-re-Forethought-21">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On July 5, 1984, Forethought hired Robert Gaskins as its vice president of product development\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p51)\n       </sup>\n       to create a new application that would be especially suited to the new\n       <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">\n        graphical\n       </a>\n       personal computers, such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">\n        Microsoft Windows\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Macintosh" title="Macintosh">\n        Apple Macintosh\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brock-2017-re-PowerPoint_23-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Brock-2017-re-PowerPoint-23">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Gaskins produced his initial description of PowerPoint about a month later (August 14, 1984) in the form of a 2-page document titled "Presentation Graphics for Overhead Projection."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-presenter-proposal_24-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-presenter-proposal-24">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       By October 1984 Gaskins had selected Dennis Austin to be the developer for PowerPoint.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">\n        <a href="#cite_note-25">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Gaskins and Austin worked together on the definition and design of the new product for nearly a year, and produced the first specification document dated August 21, 1985.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">\n        <a href="#cite_note-26">\n         [26]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This first design document showed a product as it would look in Microsoft\n       <a href="/wiki/Windows_1.0" title="Windows 1.0">\n        Windows 1.0\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">\n        <a href="#cite_note-27">\n         [27]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       which at that time had not been released.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">\n        <a href="#cite_note-28">\n         [28]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Development from that spec was begun by Austin in November 1985, for Macintosh first.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p104)\n       </sup>\n       About six months later, on May 1, 1986, Gaskins and Austin chose a second developer to join the project, Thomas Rudkin.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p149)\n       </sup>\n       Gaskins prepared two final product specification marketing documents in June 1986; these described a product for both Macintosh and Windows.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">\n        <a href="#cite_note-29">\n         [29]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">\n        <a href="#cite_note-30">\n         [30]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       At about the same time, Austin, Rudkin, and Gaskins produced a second and final major design specification document, this time showing a Macintosh look.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">\n        <a href="#cite_note-31">\n         [31]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Throughout this development period the product was called "Presenter." Then, just before release, there was a last-minute check with Forethought\'s lawyers to register the name as a trademark, and "Presenter" was unexpectedly rejected because it had already been used by someone else. Gaskins says that he thought of "PowerPoint", based on the product\'s goal of "empowering" individual presenters, and sent that name to the lawyers for clearance, while all the documentation was hastily revised.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Indezine-2012_32-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Indezine-2012-32">\n         [32]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Funding to complete development of PowerPoint was assured in mid-January, 1987, when a new Apple Computer venture capital fund, called Apple\'s Strategic Investment Group,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">\n        <a href="#cite_note-33">\n         [33]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       selected PowerPoint to be its first investment.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (pp169\xe2\x80\x93171)\n       </sup>\n       A month later, on February 22, 1987, Forethought announced PowerPoint at the Personal Computer Forum in Phoenix;\n       <a href="/wiki/John_Sculley" title="John Sculley">\n        John Sculley\n       </a>\n       , the CEO of Apple, appeared at the announcement and said "We see desktop presentation as potentially a bigger market for Apple than desktop publishing."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">\n        <a href="#cite_note-34">\n         [34]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint 1.0 for Macintosh shipped from manufacturing on April 20, 1987, and the first production run of 10,000 units was sold out.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">\n        <a href="#cite_note-35">\n         [35]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Acquisition_by_Microsoft_(1987\xe2\x80\x931992)">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition_by_Microsoft_.281987.E2.80.931992.29">\n        Acquisition by Microsoft (1987\xe2\x80\x931992)\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Acquisition by Microsoft (1987\xe2\x80\x931992)">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       By early 1987, Microsoft was starting to plan a new application to create presentations, an activity led by\n       <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Raikes" title="Jeff Raikes">\n        Jeff Raikes\n       </a>\n       , who was head of marketing for the Applications Division.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_36-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Microsoft assigned an internal group to write a specification and plan for a new presentation product.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">\n        <a href="#cite_note-37">\n         [37]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       They contemplated an acquisition to speed up development, and in early 1987 Microsoft sent a letter of intent to acquire\n       <a href="/wiki/Dave_Winer" title="Dave Winer">\n        Dave Winer\n       </a>\n       \'s product called\n       <a href="/wiki/MORE_(application)" title="MORE (application)">\n        MORE\n       </a>\n       , an outlining program that could print its outlines as bullet charts.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">\n        <a href="#cite_note-38">\n         [38]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       During this preparatory activity Raikes discovered that a program specifically to make overhead presentations was already being developed by Forethought, Inc., and that it was nearly completed.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_36-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Raikes and others visited Forethought on February 6, 1987, for a confidential demonstration.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p173)\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Raikes later recounted his reaction to seeing PowerPoint and his report about it to\n       <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">\n        Bill Gates\n       </a>\n       , who was initially skeptical:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_36-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        I thought, "software to do overheads\xe2\x80\x94that\'s a great idea." I came back to see Bill. I said, "Bill, I think we really ought to do this;" and Bill said, "No, no, no, no, no, that\'s just a feature of Microsoft Word, just put it into Word." ... And I kept saying, "Bill, no, it\'s not just a feature of Microsoft Word, it\'s a whole genre of how people do these presentations." And, to his credit, he listened to me and ultimately allowed me to go forward and ... buy this company in Silicon Valley called Forethought, for the product known as PowerPoint.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       When PowerPoint was released by Forethought, its initial press was favorable; the\n       <i>\n        Wall Street Journal\n       </i>\n       reported on early reactions:\n       <span style="padding-right:.2em;">\n        "\n       </span>\n       \'I see about one product a year I get this excited about,\' says Amy Wohl, a consultant in Bala Cynwyd, Pa. \'People will buy a Macintosh just to get access to this product.\n       <span style="padding-right:.2em;">\n        \'\n       </span>\n       "\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">\n        <a href="#cite_note-39">\n         [39]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On April 28, 1987, a week after shipment, a group of Microsoft\'s senior executives spent another day at Forethought to hear about initial PowerPoint sales on Macintosh and plans for Windows.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p191)\n       </sup>\n       The following day, Microsoft sent a letter to Dave Winer withdrawing its earlier letter of intent to acquire his company,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">\n        <a href="#cite_note-40">\n         [40]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and in mid-May 1987 Microsoft sent a letter of intent to acquire Forethought.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">\n        <a href="#cite_note-41">\n         [41]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       As requested in that letter of intent, Robert Gaskins from Forethought went to Redmond for a one-on-one meeting with Bill Gates in early June, 1987,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history_22-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-history-22">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p197)\n       </sup>\n       and by the end of July an agreement was concluded for an acquisition. The\n       <i>\n        New York Times\n       </i>\n       reported:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">\n        <a href="#cite_note-42">\n         [42]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        ... July 30\xe2\x80\x94 The Microsoft Corporation announced its first significant software acquisition today, paying $14\xc2\xa0million [$29.5\xc2\xa0million in present-day terms\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_43-0">\n         <a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-43">\n          [43]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        ] for Forethought Inc. of Sunnyvale, Calif. Forethought makes a program called PowerPoint that allows users of Apple Macintosh computers to make overhead transparencies or flip charts. ... [T]he acquisition of Forethought is the first significant one for Microsoft, which is based in Redmond, Wash. Forethought would remain in Sunnyvale, giving Microsoft a Silicon Valley presence. The unit will be headed by Robert Gaskins, Forethought\'s vice president of product development.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       Microsoft\'s president\n       <a href="/wiki/Jon_Shirley" title="Jon Shirley">\n        Jon Shirley\n       </a>\n       offered Microsoft\'s motivation for the acquisition:\n       <span style="padding-right:.2em;">\n        "\n       </span>\n       \'We made this deal primarily because of our belief in desktop presentations as a product category. ... Forethought was first to market with a product in this category.\n       <span style="padding-right:.2em;">\n        \'\n       </span>\n       "\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shirley-comments_44-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Shirley-comments-44">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Microsoft set up within its Applications Division an independent "Graphics Business Unit" to develop and to market PowerPoint, the first Microsoft application group distant from the main Redmond location.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shirley-comments_44-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Shirley-comments-44">\n         [44]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       All the PowerPoint people from Forethought joined Microsoft, and the new location was headed by Robert Gaskins, with Dennis Austin and Thomas Rudkin leading development.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBU-first-year-1988_45-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-GBU-first-year-1988-45">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       PowerPoint 1.0 for Macintosh was modified to indicate the new Microsoft ownership and continued to be sold.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBU-first-year-1988_45-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-GBU-first-year-1988-45">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A new PowerPoint 2.0 for Macintosh, adding color 35mm slides, appeared by mid-1988,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBU-first-year-1988_45-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-GBU-first-year-1988-45">\n         [45]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and again won good reviews.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pournelle198901_46-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-pournelle198901-46">\n         [46]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The same PowerPoint 2.0 product re-developed for Windows was shipped two years later, in mid-1990, at the same time as\n       <a href="/wiki/Windows_3.0" title="Windows 3.0">\n        Windows 3.0\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">\n        <a href="#cite_note-47">\n         [47]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Much of the color technology was the fruit of a joint development partnership with\n       <a href="/wiki/Genigraphics" title="Genigraphics">\n        Genigraphics\n       </a>\n       , at that time the dominant presentation services company.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GBU-first-year-genigraphics_48-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-GBU-first-year-genigraphics-48">\n         [48]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint 3.0, which was shipped in 1992 for both Windows and Mac, added live video for projectors and monitors, with the result that PowerPoint was thereafter used for delivering presentations as well as for preparing them. This was at first an alternative to overhead transparencies and 35mm slides, but over time would come to replace them.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CACM-2007-Gaskins-history_49-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-CACM-2007-Gaskins-history-49">\n         [49]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Part_of_Microsoft_Office_(since_1993)">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Part_of_Microsoft_Office_.28since_1993.29">\n        Part of Microsoft Office (since 1993)\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Part of Microsoft Office (since 1993)">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Microsoft_Office" title="History of Microsoft Office">\n        History of Microsoft Office\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint had been included in\n       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office" title="Microsoft Office">\n        Microsoft Office\n       </a>\n       from the beginning. PowerPoint 2.0 for Macintosh was part of the first Office bundle for Macintosh which was offered in mid-1989.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mac-office-1989_50-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mac-office-1989-50">\n         [50]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       When PowerPoint 2.0 for Windows appeared, a year later, it was part of a similar Office bundle for Windows, which was offered in late 1990.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-win-office-1990_51-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-win-office-1990-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Both of these were bundling promotions, in which the independent applications were packaged together and offered for a lower total price.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mac-office-1989_50-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mac-office-1989-50">\n         [50]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-win-office-1990_51-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-win-office-1990-51">\n         [51]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint 3.0 (1992) was again separately specified and developed,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001-hist_52-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-hist-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and was prominently advertised and sold separately from Office.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">\n        <a href="#cite_note-53">\n         [53]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It was, as before, included in\n       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_3.0" title="Microsoft Office 3.0">\n        Microsoft Office 3.0\n       </a>\n       , both for Windows and the corresponding version for Macintosh.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">\n        <a href="#cite_note-54">\n         [54]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A plan to integrate the applications themselves more tightly had been indicated as early as February 1991, toward the end of PowerPoint 3.0 development, in an internal memo by Bill Gates:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">\n        <a href="#cite_note-55">\n         [55]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        Another important question is what portion of our applications sales over time will be a set of applications versus a single product. ... Please assume that we stay ahead in integrating our family together in evaluating our future strategies\xe2\x80\x94the product teams WILL deliver on this. ... I believe that we should position the "OFFICE" as our most important application.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       The move from bundling separate products to integrated development began with PowerPoint 4.0, developed in 1993\xe2\x80\x931994 under new management from Redmond.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">\n        <a href="#cite_note-56">\n         [56]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The PowerPoint group in Silicon Valley was reorganized from the independent "Graphics Business Unit" (GBU) to become the "Graphics Product Unit" (GPU) for Office, and PowerPoint 4.0 changed to adopt a converged user interface and other components shared with the other apps in Office.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001-hist_52-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-hist-52">\n         [52]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       When it was released, the computer press reported on the change approvingly: "PowerPoint 4.0 has been re-engineered from the ground up to resemble and work with the latest applications in Office: Word 6.0, Excel 5.0, and Access 2.0. The integration is so good, you\'ll have to look twice to make sure you\'re running PowerPoint and not Word or Excel."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">\n        <a href="#cite_note-57">\n         [57]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Office integration was further underscored in the following version, PowerPoint 95, which was given the version number PowerPoint 7.0 (skipping 5.0 and 6.0) so that all the components of Office would share the same major version number.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">\n        <a href="#cite_note-58">\n         [58]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Although PowerPoint by this point had become part of the integrated Microsoft Office product, its development remained in Silicon Valley. Succeeding versions of PowerPoint introduced important changes, particularly version 12.0 (2007) which had a very different shared Office "\n       <a href="/wiki/Ribbon_(computing)" title="Ribbon (computing)">\n        ribbon\n       </a>\n       " user interface, and a new shared\n       <a href="/wiki/Office_Open_XML" title="Office Open XML">\n        Office XML-based file format\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">\n        <a href="#cite_note-59">\n         [59]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This marked the 20th anniversary of PowerPoint, and Microsoft held an event to commemorate that anniversary at its Silicon Valley Campus for the PowerPoint team there. Special guests were Robert Gaskins, Dennis Austin, and Thomas Rudkin, and the featured speaker was Jeff Raikes, all from PowerPoint 1.0 days, 20 years before.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">\n        <a href="#cite_note-60">\n         [60]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Since then major development of PowerPoint as part of Office has continued. New development techniques\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">\n        <a href="#cite_note-61">\n         [61]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       (shared across Office) for PowerPoint 2016 have made it possible to ship versions of PowerPoint 2016 for Windows, Mac, iOS, Android, and web access nearly simultaneously,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crowley_19-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Crowley-19">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and to release new features on a nearly-monthly schedule.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">\n        <a href="#cite_note-62">\n         [62]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       PowerPoint development is still located in Silicon Valley as of 2017\n       <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">\n        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit">\n         [update]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">\n        <a href="#cite_note-63">\n         [63]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 2010, Jeff Raikes, who had most recently been President of the Business Division of Microsoft (including responsibility for Office),\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">\n        <a href="#cite_note-64">\n         [64]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       observed: "of course, today we know that PowerPoint is often times the number two\xe2\x80\x94or in some cases even the number one\xe2\x80\x94most-used tool" among the applications in Office.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_36-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sales_and_market_share">\n        Sales and market share\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sales and market share">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint\'s initial sales were about 40,000 copies sold in 1987 (nine months), about 85,000 copies in 1988, and about 100,000 copies in 1989, all for Macintosh.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_65-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       PowerPoint\'s market share in its first three years was a tiny part of the total presentation market, which was very heavily dominated by\n       <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">\n        MS-DOS\n       </a>\n       applications on PCs.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PC-market-share-2005_66-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-PC-market-share-2005-66">\n         [66]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The market leaders on MS-DOS in 1988-1989\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">\n        <a href="#cite_note-67">\n         [67]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       were\n       <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Graphics" title="Harvard Graphics">\n        Harvard Graphics\n       </a>\n       (introduced by\n       <a href="/wiki/Software_Publishing_Corporation" title="Software Publishing Corporation">\n        Software Publishing\n       </a>\n       in 1986\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">\n        <a href="#cite_note-68">\n         [68]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       ) in first place, and\n       <a href="/wiki/IBM_Lotus_Freelance_Graphics" title="IBM Lotus Freelance Graphics">\n        Lotus Freelance Plus\n       </a>\n       (also introduced in 1986\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">\n        <a href="#cite_note-69">\n         [69]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       ) as a strong second.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">\n        <a href="#cite_note-70">\n         [70]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       They were competing with more than a dozen other MS-DOS presentation products,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">\n        <a href="#cite_note-71">\n         [71]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and Microsoft did not develop a PowerPoint version for MS-DOS.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">\n        <a href="#cite_note-72">\n         [72]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       After three years, PowerPoint sales were disappointing. Jeff Raikes, who had bought PowerPoint for Microsoft, later recalled: "By 1990, it looked like it wasn\'t a very smart idea [for Microsoft to have acquired PowerPoint], because not very many people were using PowerPoint."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist_36-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-raikes-history-pt2-2010-hist-36">\n         [36]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       This began to change when the first version for Windows, PowerPoint 2.0, brought sales up to about 200,000 copies in 1990 and to about 375,000 copies in 1991, with Windows units outselling Macintosh.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_65-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p403)\n       </sup>\n       PowerPoint sold about 1\xc2\xa0million copies in 1992, of which about 80 percent were for Windows and about 20 percent for Macintosh,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_65-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p403)\n       </sup>\n       and in 1992 PowerPoint\'s market share of worldwide presentation graphics software sales was reported as 63 percent.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_65-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p404)\n       </sup>\n       By the last six months of 1992, PowerPoint revenue was running at a rate of over $100\xc2\xa0million annually ($211\xc2\xa0million in present-day terms\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_43-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-43">\n         [43]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       ).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_65-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p405)\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">\n        <a href="#cite_note-73">\n         [73]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Sales of PowerPoint 3.0 doubled to about 2\xc2\xa0million copies in 1993, of which about 90 percent were for Windows and about 10 percent for Macintosh,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_65-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p403)\n       </sup>\n       and in 1993 PowerPoint\'s market share of worldwide presentation graphics software sales was reported as 78 percent.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_65-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p404)\n       </sup>\n       In both years, about half of total revenue came from sales outside the U.S.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums_65-7">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-salesnums-65">\n         [65]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p404)\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       By 1997 PowerPoint sales had doubled again, to more than 4\xc2\xa0million copies annually, representing 85 percent of the world market.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">\n        <a href="#cite_note-74">\n         [74]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Also in 1997, an internal publication from the PowerPoint group said that by then over 20\xc2\xa0million copies of PowerPoint were in use, and that total revenues from PowerPoint over its first ten years (1987 to 1996) had already exceeded $1\xc2\xa0billion.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">\n        <a href="#cite_note-75">\n         [75]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Since the late 1990s, PowerPoint\'s market share of total world presentation software has been estimated at 95 percent by both industry and academic sources.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thielsch-Perabo-2012-history_76-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Thielsch-Perabo-2012-history-76">\n         [76]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Operation">\n        Operation\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Operation">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       The earliest version of PowerPoint (1987 for Macintosh) could be used to print black and white pages to be photocopied onto\n       <a href="/wiki/Transparency_(projection)" title="Transparency (projection)">\n        sheets of transparent film\n       </a>\n       for projection from\n       <a href="/wiki/Overhead_projector" title="Overhead projector">\n        overhead projectors\n       </a>\n       , and to print speaker\'s notes and audience handouts; the next version (1988 for Macintosh, 1990 for Windows) was extended to also produce\n       <a href="/wiki/Presentation_slide" title="Presentation slide">\n        color 35mm slides\n       </a>\n       by communicating a file over a modem to a\n       <a href="/wiki/Genigraphics" title="Genigraphics">\n        Genigraphics\n       </a>\n       imaging center with slides returned by overnight delivery for projection from\n       <a href="/wiki/Slide_projector" title="Slide projector">\n        slide projectors\n       </a>\n       . PowerPoint was used for planning and preparing a presentation, but not for delivering it (apart from previewing it on a computer screen, or distributing printed paper copies).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CACM-2007-Gaskins-operation_77-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-CACM-2007-Gaskins-operation-77">\n         [77]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The operation of PowerPoint changed substantially in its third version (1992 for Windows and Macintosh), when PowerPoint was extended to also deliver a presentation by producing direct video output to\n       <a href="/wiki/Video_projector" title="Video projector">\n        digital projectors\n       </a>\n       or large monitors.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CACM-2007-Gaskins-operation_77-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-CACM-2007-Gaskins-operation-77">\n         [77]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In 1992 video projection of presentations was rare and expensive, and practically unknown from a laptop computer. Robert Gaskins, one of the creators of PowerPoint, says he publicly demonstrated that use for the first time at a large Microsoft meeting held in Paris on February 25, 1992, by using an unreleased development build of PowerPoint 3.0 running on an early pre-production sample of a powerful new color laptop and feeding a\n       <a href="/wiki/Talaria_projector" title="Talaria projector">\n        professional auditorium video projector\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-Gaskins-operation_78-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-Gaskins-operation-78">\n         [78]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (pp373\xe2\x80\x93375)\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       By about 2003, ten years later, digital projection had become the dominant mode of use, replacing transparencies and 35mm slides and their projectors.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweating-Bullets-2012-Gaskins-operation_78-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Sweating-Bullets-2012-Gaskins-operation-78">\n         [78]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (pp410\xe2\x80\x93414)\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">\n        <a href="#cite_note-79">\n         [79]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       As a result, the meaning of "PowerPoint presentation" narrowed to mean specifically digital projection:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007_80-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007-80">\n         [80]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        ... in the business lexicon, "PowerPoint presentation" had come to refer to a presentation made using a PowerPoint slideshow projected from a computer. Although the PowerPoint software had been used to generate transparencies for over a decade, this usage was not typically encompassed by common understanding of the term.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       In contemporary operation, PowerPoint is used to create a file (called a "presentation" or "deck"\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">\n        <a href="#cite_note-81">\n         [81]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       ) containing a sequence of pages (called "slides" in the app) which usually have a consistent style (from template masters), and which may contain information imported from other apps or created in PowerPoint, including text, bullet lists, tables, charts, drawn shapes, images, audio clips, video clips, animations of elements, and animated transitions between slides, plus attached notes for each slide.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">\n        <a href="#cite_note-82">\n         [82]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       After such a file is created, typical operation is to present it as a\n       <a href="/wiki/Slide_show" title="Slide show">\n        slide show\n       </a>\n       using a portable computer, where the presentation file is stored on the computer or available from a network, and the computer\'s screen shows a "presenter view" with current slide, next slide, speaker\'s notes for the current slide, and other information.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">\n        <a href="#cite_note-83">\n         [83]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Video is sent from the computer to one or more external digital projectors or monitors, showing only the current slide to the audience, with sequencing controlled by the speaker at the computer. A smartphone remote control built in to PowerPoint for iOS (optionally controlled from Apple Watch)\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">\n        <a href="#cite_note-84">\n         [84]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and for Android\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">\n        <a href="#cite_note-85">\n         [85]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       allows the presenter to control the show from elsewhere in the room.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In addition to a computer slide show projected to a live audience by a speaker, PowerPoint can be used to deliver a presentation in a number of other ways:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        Displayed on the screen of the presentation computer or tablet (for a very small group)\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">\n         <a href="#cite_note-86">\n          [86]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Printed for distribution as paper documents (in several formats)\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">\n         <a href="#cite_note-87">\n          [87]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Distributed as files for private viewing, even on computers without PowerPoint\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">\n         <a href="#cite_note-88">\n          [88]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Packaged for distribution on CD or a network, including linked and embedded data\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">\n         <a href="#cite_note-89">\n          [89]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Transmitted as a live broadcast presentation over the web\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">\n         <a href="#cite_note-90">\n          [90]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Embeded in a web page or blog\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">\n         <a href="#cite_note-91">\n          [91]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Shared on social networks such as\n        <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">\n         Facebook\n        </a>\n        or\n        <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">\n         Twitter\n        </a>\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">\n         <a href="#cite_note-92">\n          [92]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Set up as a self-running unattended display\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">\n         <a href="#cite_note-93">\n          [93]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Recorded as video/audio (\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H.264" title="H.264">\n         H.264\n        </a>\n        /\n        <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Audio_Coding" title="Advanced Audio Coding">\n         AAC\n        </a>\n        ), to be distributed as for any other video\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">\n         <a href="#cite_note-94">\n          [94]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       Some of these ways of using PowerPoint have been studied by\n       <a href="/wiki/JoAnne_Yates" title="JoAnne Yates">\n        JoAnne Yates\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Wanda_Orlikowski" title="Wanda Orlikowski">\n        Wanda Orlikowski\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/MIT_Sloan_School_of_Management" title="MIT Sloan School of Management">\n        MIT Sloan School of Management\n       </a>\n       :\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007_80-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007-80">\n         [80]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        The standard form of such presentations involves a single person standing before a group of people, talking and using the PowerPoint slideshow to project visual aids onto a screen. ... In practice, however, presentations are not always delivered in this mode. In our studies, we often found that the presenter sat at a table with a small group of people and walked them through a "deck", composed of paper copies of the slides. In some cases, decks were simply distributed to individuals, without even a walk-through or discussion. ... Other variations in form included sending the PowerPoint file electronically to another site and talking through the slides over an audio or video channel (e.g., telephone or video conference) as both parties viewed the slides. ... Another common variation was placing a PowerPoint file on a web site for people to view at different times.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       They found that some of these ways of using PowerPoint could influence the content of presentations, for example when "the slides themselves have to carry more of the substance of the presentation, and thus need considerably more content than they would have if they were intended for projection by a speaker who would orally provide additional details and nuance about content and context."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007_80-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Yates-and-Orlikowski-2007-80">\n         [80]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">\n        Cultural impact\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cultural impact">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Presentation_in_Progress.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Presentation_in_Progress.jpg/220px-Presentation_in_Progress.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Presentation_in_Progress.jpg/330px-Presentation_in_Progress.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Presentation_in_Progress.jpg/440px-Presentation_in_Progress.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Presentation_in_Progress.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         A PowerPoint presentation in progress\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint, more than most other personal computer applications, has been experienced as a powerful force producing change throughout all of society. In 2016 an analyst summed up: "the real mystery is ... \'how come almost every organisation in the world is using PowerPoint to communicate almost everything to almost everybody?\'. That\'s the real question. How come PowerPoint is everywhere?"\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies_14-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Davies-14">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Business_uses">\n        Business uses\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Business uses">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint was originally targeted just for business presentations. Robert Gaskins, who was responsible for its design, has written about his intended customers: "... I did not target other existing large groups of users of presentations, such as school teachers or military officers. ... I also did not plan to target people who were not existing users of presentations ... such as clergy and school children ... . Our focus was purely on business users, in small and large companies, from one person to the largest multinationals."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaskins-Sweating-Bullets-2012-cultural_95-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Gaskins-Sweating-Bullets-2012-cultural-95">\n         [95]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (pp76\xe2\x80\x9377)\n       </sup>\n       Business people had for a long time made presentations for sales calls and for internal company communications, and PowerPoint produced the same formats in the same style and for the same purposes.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaskins-Sweating-Bullets-2012-cultural_95-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Gaskins-Sweating-Bullets-2012-cultural-95">\n         [95]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p420)\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint use in business grew over its first five years (1987-1992) to sales of about 1 million copies annually, for worldwide market share of 63 percent.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">\n        <a href="#cite_note-96">\n         [96]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Over the following five years (1992-1997) PowerPoint sales accelerated, to a rate of about 4 million copies annually, for worldwide market share of 85 percent.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">\n        <a href="#cite_note-97">\n         [97]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The increase in business use has been attributed to "\n       <a href="/wiki/Network_effect" title="Network effect">\n        network effects\n       </a>\n       ," whereby additional users of PowerPoint in a company or an industry increased its salience and value to other users.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">\n        <a href="#cite_note-98">\n         [98]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Not everyone immediately approved of the greater use of PowerPoint for presentations, even in business. CEOs who very early were reported to discourage or ban PowerPoint presentations at internal business meetings included\n       <a href="/wiki/Louis_V._Gerstner_Jr." title="Louis V. Gerstner Jr.">\n        Lou Gerstner\n       </a>\n       (at IBM, in 1993),\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">\n        <a href="#cite_note-99">\n         [99]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Scott_McNealy" title="Scott McNealy">\n        Scott McNealy\n       </a>\n       (at Sun Microsystems, in 1996),\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">\n        <a href="#cite_note-100">\n         [100]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Steve_Jobs" title="Steve Jobs">\n        Steve Jobs\n       </a>\n       (at Apple, in 1997).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">\n        <a href="#cite_note-101">\n         [101]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       But even so, Rich Gold, a scholar who studied corporate presentation use at\n       <a href="/wiki/PARC_(company)" title="PARC (company)">\n        Xerox PARC\n       </a>\n       , could write in 1999: "Within today\'s corporation, if you want to communicate an idea ... you use PowerPoint."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">\n        <a href="#cite_note-102">\n         [102]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Uses_beyond_business">\n        Uses beyond business\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Uses beyond business">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       At the same time that PowerPoint was becoming dominant in business settings, it was also being adopted for uses beyond business: "Personal computing ... scaled up the production of presentations. ... The result has been the rise of presentation culture. In an information society, nearly everyone presents."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">\n        <a href="#cite_note-103">\n         [103]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 1998, at about the same time that Gold was pronouncing PowerPoint\'s ubiquity in business, the influential\n       <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">\n        Bell Labs\n       </a>\n       engineer\n       <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Lucky" title="Robert W. Lucky">\n        Robert W. Lucky\n       </a>\n       could already write about broader uses:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">\n        <a href="#cite_note-104">\n         [104]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        ... the world has run amok with the giddy power of presentation graphics. A new language is in the air, and it is codified in PowerPoint. ... In a family discussion about what to do on a given evening, for example, I feel like pulling out my laptop and giving a\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viewgraph" title="Viewgraph">\n         Vugraph\n        </a>\n        presentation ... . In church I am surprised that the preachers haven\'t caught on yet. ... How have we gotten on so long without PowerPoint?\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       Over a decade or so, beginning in the mid 1990s, PowerPoint began to be used in many communication situations, well beyond its original business presentation uses, to include teaching in schools\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">\n        <a href="#cite_note-105">\n         [105]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and in universities,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">\n        <a href="#cite_note-106">\n         [106]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       lecturing in scientific meetings\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">\n        <a href="#cite_note-107">\n         [107]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       (and preparing their related poster sessions\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">\n        <a href="#cite_note-108">\n         [108]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       ), worshipping in churches,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">\n        <a href="#cite_note-109">\n         [109]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       making legal arguments in courtrooms,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">\n        <a href="#cite_note-110">\n         [110]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       displaying supertitles in theaters,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">\n        <a href="#cite_note-111">\n         [111]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       driving helmet-mounted displays in spacesuits for NASA astronauts,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">\n        <a href="#cite_note-112">\n         [112]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       giving military briefings,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">\n        <a href="#cite_note-113">\n         [113]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       issuing governmental reports,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">\n        <a href="#cite_note-114">\n         [114]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       undertaking diplomatic negotiations,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">\n        <a href="#cite_note-115">\n         [115]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">\n        <a href="#cite_note-116">\n         [116]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       writing novels,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">\n        <a href="#cite_note-117">\n         [117]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       giving architectural demonstrations,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">\n        <a href="#cite_note-118">\n         [118]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       prototyping website designs,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">\n        <a href="#cite_note-119">\n         [119]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       creating animated video games,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">\n        <a href="#cite_note-120">\n         [120]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       creating art projects,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vienne_121-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Vienne-121">\n         [121]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and even as a substitute for writing engineering technical reports,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">\n        <a href="#cite_note-122">\n         [122]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and as an organizing tool for writing general business documents.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">\n        <a href="#cite_note-123">\n         [123]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       By 2003, it seemed that PowerPoint was being used everywhere. Julia Keller reported for the\n       <i>\n        Chicago Tribune\n       </i>\n       :\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">\n        <a href="#cite_note-124">\n         [124]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        PowerPoint ... is one of the most pervasive and ubiquitous technological tools ever concocted. In less than a decade, it has revolutionized the worlds of business, education, science and communications, swiftly becoming the standard for just about anybody who wants to explain just about anything to just about anybody else. From corporate middle managers reporting on production goals to 4th-graders fashioning a show-and-tell on the French and Indian War to church pastors explicating the seven deadly sins ... PowerPoint seems poised for world domination.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_reactions">\n        Cultural reactions\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cultural reactions">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       As uses broadened, cultural awareness of PowerPoint grew and commentary about it began to appear. "With the widespread adoption of PowerPoint came complaints ... often very general statements reflecting dissatisfaction with modern media and communication practices as well as the dysfunctions of organizational culture."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">\n        <a href="#cite_note-125">\n         [125]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Indications of this awaremess included increasing mentions of PowerPoint use in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Dilbert" title="Dilbert">\n        Dilbert\n       </a>\n       comic strips of\n       <a href="/wiki/Scott_Adams" title="Scott Adams">\n        Scott Adams\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">\n        <a href="#cite_note-126">\n         [126]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       comic parodies of poor or inappropriate use such as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg_Address" title="Gettysburg Address">\n        Gettysburg Address\n       </a>\n       in PowerPoint\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">\n        <a href="#cite_note-127">\n         [127]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">\n        <a href="#cite_note-128">\n         [128]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       or summaries of Shakespeare\'s\n       <i>\n        Hamlet\n       </i>\n       and Nabokov\'s\n       <i>\n        Lolita\n       </i>\n       in PowerPoint,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">\n        <a href="#cite_note-129">\n         [129]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and a vast number of publications on the general subject of PowerPoint, especially about how to use it.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">\n        <a href="#cite_note-130">\n         [130]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Out of all the analyses of PowerPoint over a quarter of a century, at least three general themes emerged as categories of reaction to its broader use: (1) "Use it less": avoid PowerPoint in favor of alternatives, such as using more-complex graphics and written prose, or using nothing;\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       (2) "Use it differently": make a major change to a PowerPoint style that is simpler and pictorial, turning the presentation toward a performance, more like a Steve Jobs keynote;\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayer-Atkinson-2004_16-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Mayer-Atkinson-2004-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and (3) "Use it better": retain much of the conventional PowerPoint style but learn to avoid making many kinds of mistakes that can interfere with communication.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-2007_17-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-2007-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_it_less">\n        Use it less\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Use it less">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Edward_Tufte" title="Edward Tufte">\n        Edward Tufte\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       An early reaction was that the broader use of PowerPoint was a mistake, and should be reversed.
