An influential example of this came from\n       <a href="/wiki/Edward_Tufte" title="Edward Tufte">\n        Edward Tufte\n       </a>\n       , an authority on information design, who has been a professor of political science, statistics, and computer science at Princeton and Yale, but is best known for his self-published books on data visualization, which have sold nearly 2\xc2\xa0million copies as of 2014.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">\n        <a href="#cite_note-131">\n         [131]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 2003, he published a widely-read booklet titled\n       <i>\n        The Cognitive Style of PowerPoint,\n       </i>\n       revised in 2006.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Tufte found a number of problems with the "cognitive style" of PowerPoint, many of which he attributed to the standard default style templates:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        PowerPoint\'s convenience for some presenters is costly to the content and the audience. These costs arise from the\n        <i>\n         cognitive style characteristics of the standard default PP presentation:\n        </i>\n        foreshortening of evidence and thought, low spatial resolution, an intensely hierarchical single-path structure as the model for organizing every type of content, breaking up narratives and data into slides and minimal fragments, rapid temporal sequencing of thin information rather than focused spatial analysis, conspicuous chartjunk and PP Phluff, branding of slides with logotypes, a preoccupation with format not content, incompetent designs for data graphics and tables, and a smirky commercialism that turns information into a sales pitch and presenters into marketeers\n        <i>\n         [italics in original]\n        </i>\n        .\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       Tufte particularly advised against using PowerPoint for reporting scientific analyses, using as a dramatic example some slides made during the flight of the space shuttle Columbia after it had been damaged by an accident at liftoff, slides which poorly communicated the engineers\' limited understanding of what had happened.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (pp8\xe2\x80\x9314)\n       </sup>\n       For such technical presentations, and for most occasions apart from its initial domain of sales presentations, Tufte advised against using PowerPoint at all; in many situations, according to Tufte, it would be better to substitute high-resolution graphics or concise prose documents as handouts for the audience to study and discuss, providing a great deal more detail.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tufte-2003-2006_15-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Tufte-2003-2006-15">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Many commentators enthusiastically joined in Tufte\'s vivid criticism of PowerPoint uses,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">\n        <a href="#cite_note-132">\n         [132]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and at a conference held in 2013 (a decade after Tufte\'s booklet appeared) one paper claimed that "Despite all the criticism about his work, Tufte can be considered as the single most influential author in the discourse on PowerPoint. ... While his approach was not rigorous from a research perspective, his articles received wide resonance with the public at large ... ."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">\n        <a href="#cite_note-133">\n         [133]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       There were also others who disagreed with Tufte\'s assertion that the PowerPoint program reduces the quality of presenters\' thoughts:\n       <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">\n        Steven Pinker\n       </a>\n       , professor of psychology at MIT and later Harvard, had earlier argued that "If anything, PowerPoint, if used well, would ideally reflect the way we think."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">\n        <a href="#cite_note-134">\n         [134]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Pinker later reinforced this opinion: "Any general opposition to PowerPoint is just dumb, ... It\'s like denouncing lectures\xe2\x80\x94before there were awful PowerPoint presentations, there were awful scripted lectures, unscripted lectures, slide shows, chalk talks, and so on."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">\n        <a href="#cite_note-135">\n         [135]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Much of the early commentary, on all sides, was "informal" and "anecdotal", because empirical research had been limited.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">\n        <a href="#cite_note-136">\n         [136]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_it_differently">\n        Use it differently\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Use it differently">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Mayer" title="Richard E. Mayer">\n        Richard E. Mayer\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Stevenote" title="Stevenote">\n        Steve Jobs Keynotes\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       A second reaction to PowerPoint use was to say that PowerPoint can be used well, but only by substantially changing its style of use. This reaction is exemplified by\n       <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Mayer" title="Richard E. Mayer">\n        Richard E. Mayer\n       </a>\n       , a professor of psychology at the University of California, Santa Barbara, who has studied cognition and learning, particularly the design of educational multimedia, and who has published more than 500 publications, including over 30 books.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">\n        <a href="#cite_note-137">\n         [137]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Mayer\'s theme has been that "In light of the science, it is up to us to make a fundamental shift in our thinking\xe2\x80\x94we can no longer expect people to struggle to try to adapt to our PowerPoint habits. Instead, we have to change our PowerPoint habits to align with the way people learn."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayer-Atkinson-2004_16-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Mayer-Atkinson-2004-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Tufte had argued his judgment that the information density of text on PowerPoint slides was too low, perhaps only 40 words on a slide, leading to over-simplified messages;\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">\n        <a href="#cite_note-138">\n         [138]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Mayer responded that his empirical research showed exactly the opposite, that the amount of text on PowerPoint slides was usually too high, and that even fewer than 40 words on a slide resulted in "PowerPoint overload" that impeded understanding during presentations.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">\n        <a href="#cite_note-139">\n         [139]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Mayer suggested a few major changes from traditional PowerPoint formats:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayer-Atkinson-2004_16-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Mayer-Atkinson-2004-16">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        replacing brief slide titles with longer "headlines" expressing complete ideas;\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        showing more slides but simpler ones;\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        removing almost all text including nearly all bullet lists (reserving the text for the spoken narration);\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        using larger, higher-quality, and more important graphics and photographs;\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        removing all extraneous decoration, backgrounds, logos and identifications, everything but the essential message.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       Mayer\'s ideas are claimed by\n       <a href="/wiki/Carmine_Gallo" title="Carmine Gallo">\n        Carmine Gallo\n       </a>\n       to have been reflected in Steve Jobs\'s presentations: "Mayer outlined fundamental principles of multimedia design based on what scientists know about cognitive functioning. Steve Jobs\'s slides adhere to each of Mayer\'s principles ... ."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallo-2009_140-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Gallo-2009-140">\n         [140]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p92)\n       </sup>\n       Though not unique to Jobs, many people saw the style for the first time in Jobs\'s famous product introductions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">\n        <a href="#cite_note-141">\n         [141]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Steve Jobs would have been using Apple\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Keynote_(presentation_software)" title="Keynote (presentation software)">\n        Keynote\n       </a>\n       which was designed for Jobs\'s own slide shows beginning in 2003, but Gallo says that "speaking like Jobs has little to do with the type of presentation software you use (PowerPoint, Keynote,etc.) ... all the techniques apply equally to PowerPoint and Keynote."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallo-2009_140-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Gallo-2009-140">\n         [140]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (pp14,46)\n       </sup>\n       Gallo adds that "Microsoft\'s PowerPoint has one big advantage over Apple\'s Keynote presentation software\xe2\x80\x94it\'s everywhere ... it\'s safe to say that the number of Keynote presentations is miniscule in comparison with PowerPoint. Although most presentation designers who are familiar with both formats prefer to work in the more elegant Keynote system, those same designers will tell you that the majority of their client work is done in PowerPoint."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallo-2009_140-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Gallo-2009-140">\n         [140]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (p44)\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Consistent with its association with Steve Jobs\'s keynotes, a response to this style has been that it is particularly effective for "ballroom-style presentations" (as often given in conference center ballrooms) where a celebrated and practiced speaker addresses a large passive audience, but less appropriate for "conference room-style presentations" which are often recurring internal business meetings for in-depth discussion with motivated counterparts.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">\n        <a href="#cite_note-142">\n         [142]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h4>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_it_better">\n        Use it better\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Use it better">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h4>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Kosslyn" title="Stephen Kosslyn">\n        Stephen Kosslyn\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       A third reaction to PowerPoint use was to conclude that the standard style is capable of being used well, but that many small points need to be executed carefully, to avoid impeding understanding. This kind of analysis is particularly associated with\n       <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Kosslyn" title="Stephen Kosslyn">\n        Stephen Kosslyn\n       </a>\n       , a cognitive neuroscientist who specializes in the psychology of learning and visual communication, and who has been head of the department of psychology at Harvard, has been Director of Stanford\'s Center for Advanced Study in the Behavioral Sciences, and has published some 300 papers and 14 books.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">\n        <a href="#cite_note-143">\n         [143]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Kosslyn presented a set of psychological principles of "human perception, memory, and comprehension" that "appears to capture the major points of agreement among researchers."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012_144-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012-144">\n         [144]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       He reports that his experiments support the idea that that it is not intuitive or obvious how to create effective PowerPoint presentations that conform to those agreed principles, and that even small differences that might not seem significant to a presenter can produce very different results in audiences\' understanding. For this reason, Kosslyn says, users need specific education to be able to identify best ways to avoid "flaws and failures":\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012_144-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012-144">\n         [144]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        Specifically, we hypothesized and found that the psychological principles are often violated in PowerPoint slideshows across different fields ... , that some types of presentation flaws are noticeable and annoying to audience members ... , and that observers have difficulty identifying many violations in graphical displays in individual slides ... . These studies converge in painting the following picture: PowerPoint presentations are commonly flawed; some types of flaws are more common than others; flaws are not isolated to one domain or context; and, although some types of flaws annoy the audience, flaws at the level of slide design are not always obvious to an untrained observer ... .\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       The many "flaws and failures" identified were those "likely to disrupt the comprehension or memory of the material." Among the most common examples were "Bulleted items are not presented individually, growing the list from the top to the bottom," "More than four bulleted items appear in a single list," "More than two lines are used per bulleted sentence," and "Words are not large enough (i.e., greater than 20 point) to be easily seen." Among audience reactions common problems reported were "Speakers read word-for-word from notes or from the slides themselves," "The slides contained too much material to absorb before the next slide was presented," and "The main point was obscured by lots of irrelevant detail."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012_144-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012-144">\n         [144]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Kosslyn observes that these findings could help to explain why the many studies of instructional effectiveness of PowerPoint have been inconclusive and conflicting, if there were differences in the quality of the presentations tested in different studies that went unobserved because "many may feel that \'good design\' is intuitively clear."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012_144-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-et-al.3D2012-144">\n         [144]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 2007 Kosslyn wrote a book about PowerPoint, in which he suggested a very large number of fairly modest changes to PowerPoint styles and gave advice on recommended ways of using PowerPoint.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-2007_17-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-2007-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In a later second book about PowerPoint he suggested nearly 150 clarifying style changes (in fewer than 150 pages).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">\n        <a href="#cite_note-145">\n         [145]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Kosslyn summarizes:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kosslyn-2007_17-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kosslyn-2007-17">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        (pp2\xe2\x80\x933,200)\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        ... there\'s nothing fundamentally wrong with the PowerPoint program as a medium; rather, I claim that the problem lies in how it is used. ... In fact, this medium is a remarkably versatile tool that can be extraordinarily effective. ... For many purposes, PowerPoint presentations are a superior medium of communication, which is why they have become standard in so many fields.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       In 2017, an online poll of social media users in the UK was reported to show that PowerPoint "remains as popular with young tech-savvy users as it is with the Baby Boomers," with about four out of five saying that "PowerPoint was a great tool for making presentations," in part because "PowerPoint, with its capacity to be highly visual, bridges the wordy world of yesterday with the visual future of tomorrow."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">\n        <a href="#cite_note-146">\n         [146]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Also in 2017, the Managerial Communication Group of\n       <a href="/wiki/MIT_Sloan_School_of_Management" title="MIT Sloan School of Management">\n        MIT Sloan School of Management\n       </a>\n       polled their incoming MBA students, finding that "results underscore just how differently this generation communicates as compared with older workers."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit-sloan-2017_147-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mit-sloan-2017-147">\n         [147]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Fewer than half of respondents reported doing any meaningful, longer-form writing at work, and even that minority mostly did so very infrequently, but "85 percent of students named producing presentations as a meaningful part of their job responsibilities. Two-thirds report that they present on a daily or weekly basis\xe2\x80\x94so it\'s no surprise that in-person presentations is the top skill they hope to improve."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit-sloan-2017_147-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mit-sloan-2017-147">\n         [147]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       One of the researchers concluded: "We\'re not likely to see future workplaces with long-form writing. The trend is toward presentations and slides, and we don\'t see any sign of that slowing down."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit-sloan-2017_147-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mit-sloan-2017-147">\n         [147]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._military_excess">\n        U.S. military excess\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: U.S. military excess">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Use of PowerPoint by the U.S. military services began slowly, because they were invested in mainframe computers, MS-DOS PCs, and specialized military-specification graphic output devices, all of which PowerPoint did not support.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">\n        <a href="#cite_note-148">\n         [148]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       But because of the strong military tradition of presenting\n       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/briefing" title="wikt:briefing">\n        briefings\n       </a>\n       , as soon as they acquired the computers needed to run it, PowerPoint became part of the U.S. military.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">\n        <a href="#cite_note-149">\n         [149]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       By 2000, ten years after PowerPoint for Windows appeared, it was already identified as an important feature of U.S. armed forces culture, in a front-page story in the\n       <i>\n        Wall Street Journal\n       </i>\n       :\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ-Jaffe-2000_150-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-WSJ-Jaffe-2000-150">\n         [150]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        Old-fashioned slide briefings, designed to update generals on troop movements, have been a staple of the military since World War II. But in only a few short years PowerPoint has altered the landscape. Just as word processing made it easier to produce long, meandering memos, the spread of PowerPoint has unleashed a blizzard of jazzy but often incoherent visuals. Instead of drawing up a dozen slides on a legal pad and running them over to the graphics department, captains and colonels now can create hundreds of slides in a few hours without ever leaving their desks. If the spirit moves them they can build in gunfire sound effects and images that explode like land mines. ... PowerPoint has become such an ingrained part of the defense culture that it has seeped into the military lexicon. "PowerPoint Ranger" is a derogatory term for a desk-bound bureaucrat more adept at making slides than tossing grenades.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       U.S. military use of PowerPoint may have influenced its use by armed forces of other countries: "Foreign armed services also are beginning to get in on the act. \'You can\'t speak with the U.S. military without knowing PowerPoint,\' says Margaret Hayes, an instructor at National Defense University in Washington D.C., who teaches Latin American military officers how to use the software."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ-Jaffe-2000_150-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-WSJ-Jaffe-2000-150">\n         [150]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       After another 10 years, in 2010 (and again on its front page) the\n       <i>\n        New York Times\n       </i>\n       reported that PowerPoint use in the military was then "a military tool that has spun out of control":\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-Bumiller-2010_151-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-Bumiller-2010-151">\n         [151]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        Like an insurgency, PowerPoint has crept into the daily lives of military commanders and reached the level of near obsession. The amount of time expended on PowerPoint, the Microsoft presentation program of computer-generated charts, graphs and bullet points, has made it a running joke in the Pentagon and in Iraq and Afghanistan. ... Commanders say that behind all the PowerPoint jokes are serious concerns that the program stifles discussion, critical thinking and thoughtful decision-making. Not least, it ties up junior officers ... in the daily preparation of slides, be it for a Joint Staff meeting in Washington or for a platoon leader\'s pre-mission combat briefing in a remote pocket of Afghanistan.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <i>\n        New York Times\n       </i>\n       account went on to say that as a result some U.S. generals had banned the use of PowerPoint in their operations:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-Bumiller-2010_151-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-Bumiller-2010-151">\n         [151]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        "PowerPoint makes us stupid," Gen.\n        <a href="/wiki/James_Mattis" title="James Mattis">\n         James N. Mattis\n        </a>\n        of the Marine Corps, the Joint Forces commander, said this month at a military conference in North Carolina. (He spoke without PowerPoint.) Brig. Gen.\n        <a href="/wiki/H._R._McMaster" title="H. R. McMaster">\n         H. R. McMaster\n        </a>\n        , who banned PowerPoint presentations when he led the successful effort to secure the northern Iraqi city of Tal Afar in 2005, followed up at the same conference by likening PowerPoint to an internal threat. "It\'s dangerous because it can create the illusion of understanding and the illusion of control," General McMaster said in a telephone interview afterward. "Some problems in the world are not bullet-izable."\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       Several incidents, about the same time, gave wide currency to discussions by serving military officers describing excessive PowerPoint use and the organizational culture that encouraged it.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">\n        <a href="#cite_note-152">\n         [152]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">\n        <a href="#cite_note-153">\n         [153]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">\n        <a href="#cite_note-154">\n         [154]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In response to the\n       <i>\n        New York Times\n       </i>\n       story,\n       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Norvig" title="Peter Norvig">\n        Peter Norvig\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Kosslyn" title="Stephen Kosslyn">\n        Stephen M. Kosslyn\n       </a>\n       sent a joint letter to the editor stressing the institutional culture of the military: "... many military personnel bemoan the overuse and misuse of PowerPoint. ... The problem is not in the tool itself, but in the way that people use it\xe2\x80\x94which is partly a result of how institutions promote misuse.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">\n        <a href="#cite_note-155">\n         [155]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The two generals who had been mentioned in 2010 as opposing the institutional culture of excessive PowerPoint use were both in the news again in 2017, when\n       <a href="/wiki/James_Mattis" title="James Mattis">\n        James N. Mattis\n       </a>\n       became U.S. Secretary of Defense,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">\n        <a href="#cite_note-156">\n         [156]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/H._R._McMaster" title="H. R. McMaster">\n        H. R. McMaster\n       </a>\n       was appointed as U.S. National Security Advisor.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">\n        <a href="#cite_note-157">\n         [157]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Artistic_medium">\n        Artistic medium\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Artistic medium">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Musician\n       <a href="/wiki/David_Byrne" title="David Byrne">\n        David Byrne\n       </a>\n       has been using PowerPoint as a medium for art for years, producing a book and DVD and showing at galleries his PowerPoint-based artwork.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vienne_121-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Vienne-121">\n         [121]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Byrne has written: "I have been working with PowerPoint, the ubiquitous presentation software, as an art medium for a number of years. It started off as a joke (this software is a symbol of corporate salesmanship, or lack thereof) but then the work took on a life of its own as I realized I could create pieces that were moving, despite the limitations of the \'medium.\n       <span style="padding-right:.2em;">\n        \'\n       </span>\n       "\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">\n        <a href="#cite_note-158">\n         [158]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 2005 Byrne toured with a theater piece styled as a PowerPoint presentation. When he presented it in Berkeley, on March 8, 2005, the University of California news service reported: "Byrne also defended its [PowerPoint\'s] appeal as more than just a business tool\xe2\x80\x94as a medium for art and theater. His talk was titled \'I \xe2\x99\xa5 PowerPoint\' ... . Berkeley alumnus Bob Gaskins and Dennis Austin ... were in the audience ... . Eventually, Byrne said, PowerPoint could be the foundation for \'presentational theater,\' with roots in Brechtian drama and Asian puppet theater."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">\n        <a href="#cite_note-159">\n         [159]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       After that performance, Byrne described it in his own online journal: "Did the PowerPoint talk in Berkeley for an audience of IT legends and academics. I was terrified. The guys that originally turned PowerPoint into a program were there, what were THEY gonna think? ... [Gaskins] did tell me afterwards that he liked the PowerPoint as theater idea, which was a relief."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">\n        <a href="#cite_note-160">\n         [160]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The expressions "PowerPoint Art" or "\n       <a href="/wiki/PptArt" title="PptArt">\n        pptArt\n       </a>\n       " are used to define a contemporary Italian artistic movement which believes that the corporate world can be a unique and exceptional source of inspiration for the artist.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">\n        <a href="#cite_note-161">\n         [161]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">\n        <a href="#cite_note-162">\n         [162]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       They say: "The pptArt name refers to PowerPoint, the symbolic and abstract language developed by the corporate world which has become a universal and highly symbolic communication system beyond cultures and borders."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">\n        <a href="#cite_note-163">\n         [163]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The wide use of PowerPoint had, by 2010, given rise to " ... a subculture of PowerPoint enthusiasts [that] is teaching the old application new tricks, and may even be turning a dry presentation format into a full-fledged artistic medium,"\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">\n        <a href="#cite_note-164">\n         [164]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       by using\n       <a href="/wiki/PowerPoint_animation" title="PowerPoint animation">\n        PowerPoint animation\n       </a>\n       to create "games, artworks, anime, and movies."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">\n        <a href="#cite_note-165">\n         [165]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="PowerPoint_Viewer">\n        PowerPoint Viewer\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: PowerPoint Viewer">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint Viewer is the name for a series of small free application programs to be used on computers without PowerPoint installed, to view, project, or print (but not create or edit) presentations.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-view-presentation-without-ppt_166-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-view-presentation-without-ppt-166">\n         [166]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The first version was introduced with PowerPoint 3.0 in 1992, to enable electronic presentations to be projected using conference-room computers and to be freely distributed; on Windows, it took advantage of the new feature of embedding\n       <a href="/wiki/TrueType" title="TrueType">\n        TrueType\n       </a>\n       fonts within PowerPoint presentation files to make such distribution easier.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">\n        <a href="#cite_note-167">\n         [167]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The same kind of viewer app was shipped with PowerPoint 3.0 for Macintosh, also in 1992.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">\n        <a href="#cite_note-168">\n         [168]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Beginning with PowerPoint 2003, a feature called "Package for CD" automatically managed all linked video and audio files plus needed fonts when exporting a presentation to a disk or flash drive or network location,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">\n        <a href="#cite_note-169">\n         [169]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and also included a copy of a revised PowerPoint Viewer application so that the result could be presented on other PCs without installing anything.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">\n        <a href="#cite_note-170">\n         [170]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The latest version that runs on Windows "was created in conjunction with PowerPoint 2010, but it can also be used to view newer presentations created in PowerPoint 2013 and PowerPoint 2016. ... All transitions, videos and effects appear and behave the same when viewed using PowerPoint Viewer as they do when viewed in PowerPoint 2010." It supports presentations created using PowerPoint 97 and later.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-view-presentation-without-ppt_166-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-view-presentation-without-ppt-166">\n         [166]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The latest version that runs on Macintosh is PowerPoint 98 Viewer for the\n       <a href="/wiki/Classic_Mac_OS" title="Classic Mac OS">\n        Classic Mac OS\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_macOS_components#Classic" title="List of macOS components">\n        Classic Environment\n       </a>\n       , for Macs supporting System 7.5 to\n       <a href="/wiki/Mac_OS_X_Tiger" title="Mac OS X Tiger">\n        Mac OS X Tiger\n       </a>\n       (10.4).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">\n        <a href="#cite_note-171">\n         [171]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It can open presentations only from PowerPoint 3.0, 4.0, and 8.0 (PowerPoint 98), although presentations created on Mac can be opened in PowerPoint Viewer on Windows.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">\n        <a href="#cite_note-172">\n         [172]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       As of 2017\n       <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">\n        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit">\n         [update]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       , the latest versions of PowerPoint Viewer for Windows (2010)\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">\n        <a href="#cite_note-173">\n         [173]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and for Macintosh (1998)\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">\n        <a href="#cite_note-174">\n         [174]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       remain available for download.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Since PowerPoint 2013, the recommended replacement for PowerPoint Viewer has been to use any computer with a browser and network access to display any accessible PowerPoint presentation by using the free web app\n       <a href="/wiki/Office_Online" title="Office Online">\n        PowerPoint Online\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">\n        <a href="#cite_note-175">\n         [175]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Versions">\n        Versions\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span        </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         Date\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         Name\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         Version\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         System\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         Comments\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PowerPoint_2010.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="369" </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PowerPoint_2010.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Microsoft PowerPoint 2010 running on Windows 7\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:66px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Powerpoint_mac_2008_icon.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="64" data-file-width="64" height="64" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/12/Powerpoint_mac_2008_icon.png" width="64"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Powerpoint_mac_2008_icon.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Icon for PowerPoint for Mac 2008\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="258" data-file-width="387" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png/220px-Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d6/Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png/330px-Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d6/Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Microsoft_PowerPoint_for_Mac_2011.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Microsoft PowerPoint for Mac 2011\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <dl>\n       <dt>\n        PowerPoint 1.0\n       </dt>\n       <dd>\n        For Macintosh: April 1987\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-1-mac-apr-1987_176-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-ppt-1-mac-apr-1987-176">\n          [176]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        Innovations included: multiple slides in a single file, organizing slides with a slide sorter view and a title view (precursor of outline view), speakers\' notes pages attached to each slide, printing of audience handouts with multiple slides per page, text with outlining styles and full word-processor formatting, graphic shapes with attached text for drawing diagrams and tables.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001_225-0">\n         <a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-225">\n          [225]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        It also shipped with a hardbound book as its manual.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-belleville-ppthistory-2000_226-0">\n         <a href="#cite_note-belleville-ppthistory-2000-226">\n          [226]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        "It produced overhead transparencies on a black-and-white Macintosh for laser printing. Presenters could now directly control their own overheads and would no longer have to work through the person with the typewriter. PowerPoint handled the task of making the overheads all look alike; one change reformats them all. Typographic fonts were better than an Orator typeball, and charts and diagrams could be imported from MacDraw, MacPaint, and Excel, thanks to the new Mac clipboard."\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gaskins-cacm-2007_227-0">\n         <a href="#cite_note-gaskins-cacm-2007-227">\n          [227]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        System requirements: (Mac) Original Macintosh or better, System 1.0 or higher, 512K RAM.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-0">\n         <a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">\n          [228]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <dl>\n       <dt>\n        PowerPoint 2.0\n       </dt>\n       <dd>\n        For Macintosh: May 1988;\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-2-mac-may-1988_178-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-ppt-2-mac-may-1988-178">\n          [178]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        for Windows: May 1990\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-2-win-may-1990_180-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-ppt-2-win-may-1990-180">\n          [180]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        Part of\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_for_Mac" title="Microsoft Office for Mac">\n         Microsoft Office for Mac\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Windows_versions" title="Microsoft Office">\n         Microsoft Office for Windows\n        </a>\n        . Innovations included: color, more word processing features, find and replace, spell checking, color schemes for presentations, guide to color selection, ability to change color scheme retrospectively, shaded coloring for fills.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001_225-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-225">\n          [225]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        "It added color 35mm slides, transmitting the resulting file over a modem to Genigraphics for imaging on Genigraphics\' film recorders and photo processing in Genigraphics\' labs overnight. Genigraphics was the leading professional service bureau, having developed its own Digital Equipment Corp. PDP-11-based computer systems for its artists. After a short time, though, Genigraphics itself switched to PowerPoint."\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gaskins-cacm-2007_227-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-gaskins-cacm-2007-227">\n          [227]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        System requirements: (Mac) Original Macintosh or better, System 4.1 or higher, 1 MB RAM. (Windows) 286 PC or higher, Windows 3.0, 1 MB RAM.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">\n          [228]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <dl>\n       <dt>\n        PowerPoint 3.0\n       </dt>\n       <dd>\n        For Windows, May 1992;\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-3-win-may-1992_181-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-ppt-3-win-may-1992-181">\n          [181]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        for Mac: September 1992\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-3-mac-sep-1992_182-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-ppt-3-mac-sep-1992-182">\n          [182]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        Part of\n        <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_3.0" title="Microsoft Office 3.0">\n         Microsoft Office for Windows 3.0\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Mac_versions" title="Microsoft Office">\n         Microsoft Office for Mac 3.0\n        </a>\n        . Innovations included: the first application designed exclusively for the new Windows 3.1 platform, full support for TrueType fonts (new in Windows 3.1), presentation templates, editing in outline view, new drawing, including freeform tool, autoshapes, flip, rotate, scale, align, and transforming imported pictures into their drawing primitives to make them editable, transitions between slides in slide show, progressive builds, incorporating sound and video.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001_225-2">\n         <a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-225">\n          [225]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        Animations included "flying bullets" where bullet points "flew" into the slide one by one, and some degree of Pen Computing support was included.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-belleville-ppthistory-2000_226-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-belleville-ppthistory-2000-226">\n          [226]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        "It added video-out to feed the new video projectors, with effects that could replace a bank of synchronized slide projectors. This version added fades, dissolves, and other transitions, as well as animation of text and pictures, and could incorporate video clips with synchronized audio."\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gaskins-cacm-2007_227-2">\n         <a href="#cite_note-gaskins-cacm-2007-227">\n          [227]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        System requirements: (Windows) 286 PC or higher, Windows 3.1, 2 MB RAM. (Mac) Macintosh Plus or better, System 7 or higher, 4 MB RAM.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-2">\n         <a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">\n          [228]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <dl>\n       <dt>\n        PowerPoint 4.0\n       </dt>\n       <dd>\n        For Windows: February 1994;\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-4-win-feb-1994_183-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-ppt-4-win-feb-1994-183">\n          [183]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        for Mac: October 1994\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-4-mac-oct-1994_184-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-ppt-4-mac-oct-1994-184">\n          [184]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        Part of\n        <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Windows_versions" title="Microsoft Office">\n         Microsoft Office for Windows 4.0\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office#Mac_versions" title="Microsoft Office">\n         Microsoft Office for Mac 4.2\n        </a>\n        . Innovations included: autolayouts, Word tables, rehearsal mode, hidden slides, and the "AutoContent Wizard."\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-belleville-ppthistory-2000_226-2">\n         <a href="#cite_note-belleville-ppthistory-2000-226">\n          [226]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        Introduced a standard "Microsoft Office" look and feel (shared with Word and Excel), with status bar, toolbars, tooltips. Full\n        <a href="/wiki/Object_Linking_and_Embedding" title="Object Linking and Embedding">\n         OLE\n        </a>\n        2.0 with in-place activation.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-austin-timeline-2001_225-3">\n         <a href="#cite_note-austin-timeline-2001-225">\n          [225]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        System requirements: (Windows) 386 PC or higher, Windows 3.1, 8 MB RAM. (Mac) 68020 Mac or better, System 7 or higher, 8 MB RAM.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013_228-3">\n         <a href="#cite_note-bitbetter-pptsystemreq-2013-228">\n          [228]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <dl>\n       <dt>\n        PowerPoint 7.0\n       </dt>\n       <dd>\n        For Windows: July 1995\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-7-win-jul-1995_185-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-ppt-7-win-jul-1995-185">\n          [185]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n       <dd>\n        Part of\n        <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Office_95" title="Microsoft Office 95">\n         Microsoft Office for Windows 95\n        </a>\n        . Innovations included: new animation effects, real curves and textures, black and white view, autocorrect, insert symbol, meeting support features such as "Meeting Minder."\n        <sup      <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: File formats">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <table class="infobox" style="width:22em">\n       <caption style="font-size:130%; padding-bottom:0.15em;">\n        PowerPoint Presentation\n       </caption>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">\n         <a href="/wiki/Filename_extension" title="Filename extension">\n          Filename extensions\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td style="line-height:1.35em;">\n         <code>\n          .pptx, .ppt\n          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-file-formats_247-0">\n           <a href="#cite_note-file-formats-247">\n            [247]\n           </a>\n          </sup>\n         </code>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">\n         <a href="/wiki/Media_type" title="Media type">\n          Internet media\xc2\xa0type\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td style="line-height:1.35em;">\n         <code>\n          application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.presentationml.presentation, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint\n          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mime-types_248-0">\n           <a href="#cite_note-mime-types-248">\n            [248]\n           </a>\n          </sup>\n         </code>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">\n         <a href="/wiki/Uniform_Type_Identifier" title="Uniform Type Identifier">\n          Uniform Type Identifier\xc2\xa0(UTI)\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <td style="line-height:1.35em;">\n         com.microsoft.powerpoint.ppt\n         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-type-codes_249-0">\n          <a href="#cite_note-type-codes-249">\n           [249]\n          </a>\n         </sup>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">\n         Developed\xc2\xa0by\n        </th>\n        <td style="line-height:1.35em;">\n         <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">\n          Microsoft\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th scope="row" style="padding:0.2em 0;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:0.65em;">\n         Type of format\n        </th>\n        <td style="line-height:1.35em;">\n         <a href="/wiki/Presentation" title="Presentation">\n          Presentation\n         </a>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Binary_(1987\xe2\x80\x932007)">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Binary_.281987.E2.80.932007.29">\n        Binary (1987\xe2\x80\x932007)\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Binary (1987\xe2\x80\x932007)">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Early versions of PowerPoint, from 1987 through 1995 (versions 1.0 through 7.0), evolved through a sequence of binary file formats, different in each version, as functionality was added.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-early-file-compatibility_250-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-early-file-compatibility-250">\n         [250]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       That resulted in a stable binary format (called a .ppt file, like all earlier binary formats) that was shared as the default in PowerPoint 97 through PowerPoint 2003 for Windows, and in PowerPoint 98 through PowerPoint 2004 for Mac (that is, in PowerPoint versions 8.0 through 11.0).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-binary-doc_251-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ppt-binary-doc-251">\n         [251]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252">\n        <a href="#cite_note-252">\n         [252]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The specification document is actively maintained and can be freely downloaded,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppt-binary-doc_251-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-ppt-binary-doc-251">\n         [251]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       because, although no longer the default, that binary format can be read and written by some later versions of PowerPoint, including the current PowerPoint 2016.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-file-formats_247-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-file-formats-247">\n         [247]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       After the stable binary format was adopted, versions of PowerPoint continued to be able to read and write differing file formats from earlier versions.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-early-file-compatibility_250-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-early-file-compatibility-250">\n         [250]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       But beginning with PowerPoint 2007 and PowerPoint 2008 for Mac (PowerPoint version 12.0), this was the only binary format available for saving; PowerPoint 2007 (version 12.0) no longer supported saving to binary file formats used earlier than PowerPoint 97 (version 8.0), ten years before.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253">\n        <a href="#cite_note-253">\n         [253]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        Binary\n        <a href="/wiki/Filename_extension" title="Filename extension">\n         filename extensions\n        </a>\n       </b>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-file-formats_247-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-file-formats-247">\n         [247]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        .ppt, PowerPoint 97\xe2\x80\x932003 binary presentation\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .pps, PowerPoint 97\xe2\x80\x932003 binary slide show\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .pot, PowerPoint 97\xe2\x80\x932003 binary template\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        Binary\n        <a href="/wiki/Media_type" title="Media type">\n         media types\n        </a>\n       </b>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mime-types_248-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mime-types-248">\n         [248]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        .ppt, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .pps, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .pot, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Office_Open_XML_(since_2007)">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Office_Open_XML_.28since_2007.29">\n        Office Open XML (since 2007)\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microsoft_PowerPoint&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Office Open XML (since 2007)">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       The big change in PowerPoint 2007 and PowerPoint 2008 for Mac (PowerPoint version 12.0) was that the stable binary file format of 97\xe2\x80\x932003 was replaced as the default by a new\n       <a href="/wiki/Zip_(file_format)" title="Zip (file format)">\n        zipped\n       </a>\n       <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">\n        XML\n       </a>\n       -based\n       <a href="/wiki/Office_Open_XML" title="Office Open XML">\n        Office Open XML\n       </a>\n       format (.pptx files).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-open-xml-formats_254-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-open-xml-formats-254">\n         [254]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Microsoft\'s explanation of the benefits of the change included: smaller file sizes, up to 75% smaller than comparable binary documents; security, through being able to identify and exclude executable macros and personal data; less chance to be corrupted than binary formats; and easier interoperability for exchanging data among Microsoft and other business applications, all while maintaining backward compatibility.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255">\n        <a href="#cite_note-255">\n         [255]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        XML\n        <a href="/wiki/Filename_extension" title="Filename extension">\n         filename extensions\n        </a>\n       </b>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-file-formats_247-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-file-formats-247">\n         [247]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        .pptx, PowerPoint 2007 XML presentation\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .pptm, PowerPoint 2007 XML macro-enabled presentation\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .ppsx, PowerPoint 2007 XML slide show\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .ppsm, PowerPoint 2007 XML macro-enabled slide show\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .ppam, PowerPoint 2007 XML add-in\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .potx, PowerPoint 2007 XML template\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .potm, PowerPoint 2007 XML macro-enabled template\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        XML\n        <a href="/wiki/Media_type" title="Media type">\n         media types\n        </a>\n       </b>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mime-types_248-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-mime-types-248">\n         [248]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        .pptx, application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.presentationml.presentation\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .pptm, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint.presentation.macroEnabled.12\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .ppsx, application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.presentationml.slideshow\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .ppsm, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint.slideshow.macroEnabled.12\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .ppam, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint.addin.macroEnabled.12\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .potx, application/vnd.openxmlformats-officedocument.presentationml.template\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        .potm, application/vnd.ms-powerpoint.template.macroEnabled.12\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       The specification for the new format was published as an\n       <a href="/wiki/Open_standard" title="Open standard">\n        open standard\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ECMA-376" title="ECMA-376">\n        ECMA-376\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256">\n        <a href="#cite_note-256">\n         [256]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       through\n       <a href="/wiki/Ecma_International" title="Ecma International">\n        Ecma International\n       </a>\n       Technical Committee 45 (TC45).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257">\n        <a href="#cite_note-257">\n         [257]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The Ecma 376 stardard was approved in December 2006, and was submitted for standardization through\n       <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_JTC_1/SC_34" title="ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 34">\n        ISO/IEC JTC 1/SC 34\n       </a>\n       WG4 in early 2007. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Standardization_of_Office_Open_XML" title="Standardization of Office Open XML">\n        standardization process\n       </a>\n       was contentious.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258">\n        <a href="#cite_note-258">\n         [258]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It was approved as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_29500" title="ISO/IEC 29500">\n        ISO/IEC 29500\n       </a>\n       in early 2008.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259">\n        <a href="#cite_note-259">\n         [259]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Copies of the ISO/IEC standard specification are freely available, in two parts.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260">\n        <a href="#cite_note-260">\n         [260]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261">\n        <a href="#cite_note-261">\n         [261]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       These define two related standards known as "Transitional" and "Strict." The two standards were progressively adopted by PowerPoint: PowerPoint version 12.0 (2007, 2008 for Mac) could read and write Transitional format, but could neither read nor write Strict format. PowerPoint version 14.0 (2010, 2011 for Mac) could read and write Transitional, and also read but not write Strict. PowerPoint version 15.0 and later (beginning 2013, 2016 for Mac) can read and write both Transitional and Strict formats. The reason for the two variants was explained by Microsoft:\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262">\n        <a href="#cite_note-262">\n         [262]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <blockquote class="templatequote">\n       <p>\n        ... the participants in the ISO/IEC standardization process recognized two objectives with competing requirements. The first objective was for the Open XML standard to provide an XML-based file format that could fully support conversion of the billions of existing Office documents without any loss of features, content, text, layout, or other information, including embedded data. The second was to specify a file format that did not rely on Microsoft-specific data types. They created two variants of Open XML\xe2\x80\x94Transitional, which supports previously-defined Microsoft-specific data types, and Strict, which does not rely on them. Prior versions of Office [that is, 2007] have supported reading and writing Transitional Open XML, and Office 2010 can read Strict Open XML documents. With the addition of write support for Strict Open XML, Office 2013 provides full support for both variants of Open XML.\n       </p>\n      </blockquote>\n      <p>\n       The PowerPoint .pptx file format (called "\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PresentationML" title="PresentationML">\n        PresentationML\n       </a>\n       " for Presentation Markup Language) contains separate structures for all the complex parts of a PowerPoint presentation.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263">\n        <a href="#cite_note-263">\n         [263]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264">\n        <a href="#cite_note-264">\n         [264]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The specification documents run to over six\xc2\xa0thousand pages.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265">\n        <a href="#cite_note-265">\n         [265]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Because of the widespread use of PowerPoint, the standardized file formats are considered important for the long-term access to digital documents in library collections and archives, according to the U.S. Library of Congress.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266">\n        <a href="#cite_note-266">\n         [266]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint 2013 and PowerPoint 2016 provide options to set default saving to ISO/IEC 29500 Strict format, but the initial default setting remains Transitional, for compatibility with legacy features incorporating binary data in existing documents.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267">\n        <a href="#cite_note-267">\n         [267]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       PowerPoint 2013 or PowerPoint 2016 will both open and save files in the former binary format (.ppt), for compatibility with older versions of the program (but not versions older than PowerPoint 97).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-file-formats_247-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-file-formats-247">\n         [247]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268">\n        <a href="#cite_note-268">\n         [268]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In saving to older formats, these versions of PowerPoint will check to assure that no features have been introduced into the presentation which are incompatible with the older formats.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-open-xml-formats_254-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-open-xml-formats-254">\n         [254]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       PowerPoint 2013 and 2016 will also save a presentation in many other file formats, including\n       <a href="/wiki/Portable_Document_Format" title="Portable Document Format">\n        PDF\n       </a>\n       format,\n       <a href="/wiki/MPEG-4" title="MPEG-4">\n        MPEG-4\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Windows_Media_Video" title="Windows Media Video">\n        WMV\n       </a>\n       video, as a sequence of single-picture files (using image formats including\n       <a href="/wiki/GIF" title="GIF">\n        GIF\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/JPEG" title="JPEG">\n        JPEG\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Portable_Network_Graphics" title="Portable Network Graphics">\n        PNG\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/TIFF" title="TIFF">\n        TIFF\n       </a>\n       , and some older formats), and as a single presentation file in which all slides are replaced with pictures. PowerPoint will both open and save files in\n       <a href="/wiki/OpenDocument_technical_specification#Document_representation" title="OpenDocument technical specification">\n        OpenDocument Presentation\n       </a>\n       format (ODP) for compatibility.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-file-formats_247-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-file-formats-247">\n         [247]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">\n        See also\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span Retrieved\n            <span class="nowrap">\n             August 24,\n            </span>\n            2017\n           </span>\n           .\n           <q>\n            A special promotion announced last week by Microsoft Corp. enables Macintosh customers to buy four of the company\'s business applications at a 35 percent discount. The special edition, called The Microsoft Office, includes Word 4.0, Excel 2.2, PowerPoint 2.01, and Mail 1.37. The package sells for $849; if purchased separately, the programs would cost $1,310, the company said. The promotion is available until the end of the year.\n           </q>\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=InfoWorld&amp;rft.atitle=The+Microsoft+Office+Bundles+4+Programs&amp;rft.volume=11&amp;rft.issue=25&amp;rft.pages=37&amp;rft.date=1989-06-19&amp;rft.issn=0199-6649&amp;rft.aulast=Flynn&amp;rft.aufirst=Laurie&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DlzAEAAAAMBAJ%26pg%3DPA17&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMicrosoft+PowerPoint">\n           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-win-office-1990-51">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          ^\n          <a href="#cite_ref-win-office-1990_51-0">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              a\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n          <a href="#cite_ref-win-office-1990_51-1">\n           <sup>\n            <i>\n             <b>\n              b\n             </b>\n            </i>\n           </sup>\n          </a>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation news">\n           Johnston, Stuart J. The product brings to the Windows environment basically the equivalent of The Microsoft Office for Macintosh, which was announced a year ago.\n           </q>\n          </cite>\n          <span Retrieved\n            <span class="nowrap">\n             September 22,\n            </span>\n            2017\n           </span>\n           .\n           <q>\n            PowerPoint succeeded so quickly because it spread rapidly by viral transmission from user to user ... every time early adopters used our product effectively, they demonstrated its value to other potential customers. PowerPoint made it especially easy for colleagues within the same company to share materials and incorporate one another\xe2\x80\x99s slides into their presentations with automatic formatting. This created networks of cooperation that benefited everyone.\n           </q>\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Bento&amp;rft.atitle=The+Man+Who+Invented+PowerPoint&amp;rft.issue=7&amp;rft.date=2016-10&amp;rft.aulast=Gaskins&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fbento.hult.edu%2Fthe-man-who-dreamed-of-powerpoint%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMicrosoft+PowerPoint">\n           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-99">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-99">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation book">\n           <a href="/wiki/Louis_V._Gerstner_Jr." title="Louis V. Retrieved\n            <span class="nowrap">\n             September 23,\n            </span>\n            2017\n           </span>\n           .\n           <q>\n            Higher education has certainly not been immune from the growing influence of presentation software. ... Five years ago, none of our department\'s classrooms were equipped to show multimedia slides. At present, all of our classrooms have been upgraded with such technology, and faculty are actively encouraged to incorporate slides into their lectures. Our institution is certainly not alone in this trend. A large number of educators in the United States use PowerPoint in their classrooms ... [with 84 references to earlier studies].\n           </q>\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Review+of+Communication&amp;rft.atitle=Pedagogy+Meets+PowerPoint%3A+A+Research+Review+of+the+Effects+of+Computer-Generated+Slides+in+the+Classroom&amp;rft.volume=6&amp;rft.issue=1%E2%80%932&amp;rft.pages=101-123&amp;rft.date=2006-08-19&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1080%2F15358590600763383&amp;rft.issn=1535-8593&amp;rft.aulast=Levasseur&amp;rft.aufirst=David+G.&amp;rft.au=Sawyer%2C+J.+Kanan&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.tandfonline.com%2Fdoi%2Ffull%2F10.1080%2F15358590600763383&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMicrosoft+PowerPoint">\n           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-107">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-107">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation news">\n           <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">\n            Pinker, Steven\n           </a>\n           (June 10, 2010).\n           <a class="external text" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2010/06/11/opinion/11Pinker.html" rel="nofollow">\n            "Mind Over Mass Media"\n           </a>\n           . Retrieved\n            <span class="nowrap">\n             September 21,\n            </span>\n            2017\n           </span>\n           .\n           <q>\n            Because PowerPoint is so modular, it allows me to block out major themes (potential sections or chapters) and quickly see if I can generate ample ideas to support them. ... Working in slides, as opposed to one long document, helps me focus on organizing before I really begin writing. I think of the slides as index cards or sticky notes that can be arranged and rearranged until I\'m sure my thoughts are in the right order. As I write, I can easily toggle back and forth from \'Slide View\' to \'Slide Sorter\' to get a sense of the whole and the parts.\n           </q>\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" Despite the pervasiveness of PowerPoint in our culture there have been few empirical studies and most of the non-empirical work is based on casual essays and informal anecdotal reviews which very often take a polemic and overall negative position on PowerPoint, rather than conducting formal scholarship. This lack of rigorous studies and empirical research is surprising given the enormous complexity and importance of the PowerPoint tool.\n           </q>\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=conference&amp;rft.btitle=10+Years+after+Tufte%27s+%22Cognitive+Style+of+PowerPoint%22%3A+Synthesizing+its+Constraining+Qualities&amp;rft.place=London&amp;rft.pages=345-350&amp;rft.pub=IEEE&amp;rft.date=2013-07-16%2F2013-07-18&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1109%2FIV.2013.44&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4799-0834-9&amp;rft.aulast=Kernbach&amp;rft.aufirst=Sebastian&amp;rft.au=Bresciani%2C+Sabrina&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fieeexplore.ieee.org%2Fstamp%2Fstamp.jsp%3Farnumber%3D6676584&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMicrosoft+PowerPoint">\n           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-137">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-137">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation web">\n           <a class="external text" href="https://www.psych.ucsb.edu/people/faculty/mayer" rel="nofollow">\n            "Richard Mayer"\n           </a>\n           .\n           <i>\n            Psychological and Brain Sciences department, University of California at Santa Barbara, faculty directory\n           </i>\n           . 2017.\n           <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170617030504/https://www.psych.ucsb.edu/people/faculty/mayer" rel="nofollow">\n            Archived\n           </a>\n           from the original on June 17, 2017\n           <span class="reference-accessdate">\n            .
